Pudong Yuqing Ties in the Secondary School Football Championship

Yesterday's Second Final

2-2 Draw Again

—No More Finals, Each Gets the Championship—

The result of the first final of the Secondary School Sports Association's football championship between Yuqing and Pudong Middle Schools was a 1-1 draw. The two teams played the second final yesterday, and the result was still a 2-2 draw. In the first half, Yuqing took the lead with a score of 1-0. In the second half, Pudong also scored one. In extra time, Pudong scored first. In the last two minutes, Yuqing scored again, making it a 2-2 draw again. The two schools agreed to no longer hold a third final, and each received the same championship trophy.

Dividing the Spoils Equally The match started at 3 PM yesterday at the baseball field. Yuqing was on the windward side. Both teams advanced and retreated. About ten minutes after the start of the match, Yuqing advanced to outside the penalty line in front of the Pudong goal and was awarded a free kick. The goalkeeper saved it, but Yuqing's right-winger Zhang Zhonghu scored with one kick. In the second half, Pudong was on the windward side. Center-back Zhang Shi sat in the central area and continuously sent out long balls. Center forward Li Jinji charged in, and by the end of the game, each team had scored one goal. Thus, there was extra time. Pudong's Chen Chongbo took the lead and scored with one swoop, and cheers rose everywhere. Unexpectedly, in the last two minutes, Yuqing's center forward Fu Zhibin scored with one touch, and Pudong's joy returned to disappointment, resulting in a 2-2 draw.

Each Gets a Championship After the match, the leaders of the two teams and the staff of the Association discussed the date for the third final. They all believed that the time was very short, the big exams were approaching, and both sides were feeling tired. In order to avoid disappointing either side, it was decided to divide the championship trophy equally, with each team receiving one. The existing one was drawn by Yuqing, and another one will be made to give to Pudong.

Grain, Periodical Powder and Wheat, Uniformly Declining

After the opening of the powder market in the morning, each month consistently presented a state of collapse and no end. The quotation of foreign wheat was a little better in Chicago by 4 points, and there was no market in Canada. However, the atmosphere of the market, since the Japanese merchants sold foreign flour at the cheapest price in Dalian, it seems that the hearts of the majority of people will be stimulated, and due to the end of the year, most of the outsiders can’t help but end, so from this month to April, they have all broken the 1-corner mark, and it fell another 2 to 3 points from the previous day. This kind of situation is really unexpected. Then, because the market was too large, the short positions profitably replenished smoothly, and the market rebounded by 1 or 2 points. After the opening in the afternoon, this month, because it was close to the final transaction, the opening price fell, and then it was slightly better, and finally it still fell, the lowest to 2 yuan and 08 and a half points. In the long term, the market in each month was stable at first, and then it was slightly soft. As a result, this month fell 1 point and 7 cents from the previous day, January was better by 5 cents and 2 cents, April was 2 cents and a half, and May was better by 5 cents.

After the opening of the wheat market in the morning and afternoon, the market in each month showed a falling phenomenon, the quotation of foreign wheat, little change, the hearts of the people, since less activity, only the selling price of the current foreign wheat, limited by the added tax, but the pricing agreement, in fact, after the added tax, there is little relationship, after which it has been decided that each will import, the needs of the flour mill, of course, will be based on foreign wheat, but the market will not be able to expand due to the gradual downturn in the flour market, and the market situation in each month, is also cannot expand. As a result, January fell by 7 cents from the previous day, February and March fell by 7 cents, and April fell by 6 cents. The existing wheat did not move due to the family, and there was no start of cloud.

Continued Strength in the Late Rice Market

(South Shanghai) Yesterday, over thirty sources arrived, of which more than ten were late rice. The buying interest from Tianjin, Yantai, and other regional merchants continued with orders for a thousand bags. Consequently, the market showed improvement, with prices increasing by five cents. The appetite for thick japonica rice and Duyang pointed rice from various regional merchants in this area remained acceptable. However, the overall market condition was still difficult to show significant strength, with prices showing neither increase nor decrease. While there was active buying from those swapping old currency for silver and small-scale buyers, the market shape remained stable due to the lack of loosened selling pressure.

(North Shanghai) Yesterday, about forty boats arrived from various routes downriver. The off-take of various goods in the market remained sluggish. Prices for white japonica, sheep-Duyang, and port rice all remained stable. Prices for those swapping old currency for silver saw a slight increase of a few cents. Early thin rice showed a slightly tighter trend.

Yellow Soybean Sales Show No Progress

Yesterday's yellow soybean market was soft, but lower-grade goods had already fallen to three yuan, so they could be temporarily stable. The top and middle-grade goods decreased by two or three cents. Market transactions were seven or eight truckloads, purchased by factories and local merchants. Although the source of goods coming in has been little lately, it is estimated that over a dozen truckloads are available still. The sales of sand yellow soybeans were also quiet, and the market atmosphere was generally sluggish. Doudou [another type of soybean] has few or no sales.

Yellow earth silk produced in Sichuan, Hubei, and other provinces is priced lower, and its sales are gradually expanding in various silk weaving factories domestically. This month, a total of one hundred and thirty-seven bales of yellow earth silk from Hangzhou, Shaoxing, Huzhou, Jiaxing in Zhejiang, and Suzhou in Jiangsu were shipped, used to weave yellow silk crepe.

Business Sutra (52)

Overview of the Daily Necessities Market (1)

(Xi Nan)

Everyone in Shanghai knows that it is a major trading port, where all industries converge. From large import and export trade of tens of millions to small daily necessities, companies and firms are countless. It is difficult to have a statistics for that at this time. Let’s not talk about commercial research. Just about the shops for our daily needs, based on my experience, I will write it out for readers' reference. It can also be called "a fool's gain."

First, let's talk about the grain. Rice shops can be seen on every road. Rice shops also do "cut noodle" business, and some also do "horse feed", and also sell flour. Originally, rice shops purchased goods from rice firms using "sea bushels", but when they sold it, they used scales, calculating two hundred pounds of net rice as one "shi" [unit of measure], plus four pounds for the bag. However, the rice purchased, such as high-quality rice, weighs two hundred fifteen or sixteen pounds per "shi", and at least two hundred seven or eight pounds. This is the benefit of being a rice shop. Nowadays, rice firms use market bushels, and rice shops also use market scales, but if you ask them to use the old measurement, they can still do it, but the price will be higher.

Rice firms have two markets, north and south. The south market is on Dou Shi Street. Since the establishment of the new wharf on the outer road, rice firms have set up branch offices there for convenient unloading. Therefore, Dou Shi Street has only become the settlement place for the rice industry. The north market is on Guangfu Road, Zhabei, probably near the north bank of the Xinzha Bridge. They are all passed from rice-selling boatmen through distributors to rice firms, then sold by rice firms to rice shops, and then sold by rice shops to consumers. This is a general statement, but it already feels like there are many middlemen and complicated procedures. If we consider from the producer – the farmer – to the consumer, there are even more people involved in the transfer.

Beans, wheat, sesame, oil cakes, mung beans, and peanuts are collectively referred to as miscellaneous grains. Most of the firms in this group also do rice business, mostly located on Dou Shi Street. However, there are also many out-of-town merchants in the miscellaneous grain industry, mostly located near Aiguo Road and Minguo Road. Trading takes place in the morning at Cai Shu Tang on Dou Shi Street, and in the afternoon at the Miscellaneous Grain and Oil Cake Trade Association on Minguo Road, the futures are at the Miscellaneous Grain Exchange. As for the local oil factory trading, it takes place at the Oil Factory Trade Association. Flour mills are mostly located in western Shanghai Zhabei, near Suzhou River, for convenient loading and unloading of flour wheat. Their spot trading is at the Flour Trade Association, and futures are at the Flour Exchange.

In terms of eating, Shanghai is all kinds of things, nothing strange. But in general, eating simple meals often means going to the hotel alleys, now changed to mainland shopping malls. Hotel alleys have become historical terms. The side dishes in hotel alleys are famous for fried "quan zi" (pig intestines) and fried "juan cai" (fish maw filling), and payment is made in small foreign coins, which is relatively cheaper, so it is welcomed by general economical people. Before the construction of the Mainland Shopping Mall, Jiujiang Road from Zhili Road to Shandong Road almost held the authority of the restaurant industry. There was also one of the three so-called tavern industry associations at that time, Tai He Yuan. For celebratory business, it competed with Hong Yun Lou on Fada Road, both north and south. Between a certain time, this section of the road often felt crowded with traffic, making it inconvenient to walk.

The term "economic meal" has also become popular. But five or six years ago, although there were cheap meals with two pieces of salted pork, a bowl of tofu soup, and three bowls of white rice, they were not something that the general public could afford. At that time, Guangdong restaurants, apart from North Sichuan Road, mostly focused on supper snacks. Ten years ago, it cost two dimes [角，jiao] to eat a supper, and white rice could be eaten as much as you wanted without restriction. The side dishes could be chosen from the range of winter mushroom ducks, fried eggs, and fragrant sausages, etc., with two items ordered at the customer's convenience. Later, Guangdong restaurants developed day by day, and there is today's situation. The cost of supper is also several times higher than in the past.

The development of Guangdong restaurants is suspected to have a considerable relationship with the "revolution". Since Guangdong was the source of the revolution, revolutionary elements from all over traveled south. When the National Revolutionary Army marched north, the soldiers who were accustomed to the Southern flavor developed a close relationship with Guangdong side dishes. Therefore, Cantonese cuisine became popular in Shanghai, and people from Jiangsu and Zhejiang were greatly moved by the thought of the fearsome "Dragon Tiger Gate".

According to the author's memory, economic meals started at the Mei Nong Sichuan Cuisine Restaurant on Sanma Road, which was five dimes per person at that time. Later, it gradually spread to other restaurants. In recent years, vegetable rice and four-joy meat have become extremely popular. It is not necessary to say that the Dongxin Bridge area is the birthplace of rice and dishes. In other places, they compete with economic rice one after another. (To be continued)

Free Talk

"Worshiping the Foreign" and "Obsessed with the Old"

— Lin Huiyin

Dissatisfaction with the status quo is likely human nature, and the progress of society can largely be attributed to the improvement driven by dissatisfaction with the status quo. However, Chinese people often do not act this way. Even when dissatisfied with the current state, they do not seek to improve it. Instead, they look for something to rely on to forget or despise reality.

To despise oneself, one worships Europe and America as much as possible. A casual remark or action by a Westerner is seen as magical and worthy of emulation. Sometimes, there is even worship, with the desire to be that foreigner. However, since that is obviously impossible, one has no choice but to settle for second best, intentionally or unintentionally seeing oneself or simply declaring oneself as a certain foreign person from the East, or a certain foreign person number two. In the film industry, in the arts, in politics, in every field, China has such talents. While in foreign countries, there are only a few in each country, China almost has the entire complement of every country.

But that is just to say worship, just to say imitation, and the imitation is almost such that one becomes the imitated, but that does not mean understanding. To say understanding is very difficult. Even in a trivial daily matter, many now have spring locks on their doors, but few seem to carry the key in their pockets, and they still have to call at the door when they get home, regardless of the trouble. They only know that what is foreign is good, but they often do not know what is good about it.

There is also a type of person who is dissatisfied with the status quo and retreats into the past. That retreat could be ten years, twenty years, one hundred years, or one thousand years. They see how good it was back then, how detached people were, how leisurely life was, how one could search for plum blossoms in the snow, how one could invite the moon for a drink. When they are really forced to the point where they can no longer bear it, they quote a line of poetry or a few sentences of prose from the ancients to whom their hearts are attached, in order to slightly relieve the pent-up feelings in their hearts.

The status quo can be forgotten, and the worship of the foreign and the obsession with the old are the two most straightforward ways to forget the status quo. The application of these two methods can be seen as very common from the fact that the two festivals that are currently accompanying us forward, Christmas and the New Year, are generally called "Christmas" and the "Gregorian" New Year. China also originally had a birthday that was called Christmas, but now, if you just say Christmas without naming it, it will definitely not refer to the Chinese one, but will certainly refer to the birthday of Jesus, regardless of whether you believe in his teachings. And when people say they are celebrating the Gregorian New Year, they are undoubtedly still waiting for the New Year - that is the authentic New Year, so it is not necessary to use the qualifier "lunar."

On "Hidden Books"

— Chen Zizhan

"Apprentices gladly enter, writing is born from resentment," this is what I said ten years ago when I first read Tang Zhen's preface to "Hidden Books". What does "writing is born from resentment" mean? The ancient sages said, "The author of the Book of Changes must have had sorrows!" King Wen was imprisoned in Youli, and he wrote the Zhou Yi; the Yi was born from resentment. The Book of Songs says, "The gentleman makes a song, to express his sorrow." The Minor Odes have many resentful poems; poetry is born from resentment. Qu Yuan's "Li Sao" and Sima Qian's "Historical Records" were also born from resentment. Reading Sima Qian's autobiography, we can know that the so-called sages of ancient times, who left behind writings to express themselves, were all works inspired by anger and entrusted with meaning. This is where my saying that "writing is born from resentment" comes from.

As for Tang Zhen, he was a county magistrate who could not fulfill his ambitions. After resigning from office, he rented a house in the city of Wu, with only three or four rooms and bare walls. The smoke from his cooking stove often disappeared, and he would pick wolfberry leaves from discarded paintings to eat as food. Dressed in rags, he was content and happy, writing continuously. Initially, he called his book "Heng Shu," intending to weigh the world, but later, because of repeated setbacks, he renamed it "Hidden Books." In his "Great Command" chapter, he bitterly criticized the phenomena of unequal distribution of wealth and unequal happiness and suffering, even saying that all the chaos in the world arises from this. Only someone who has been hungry himself and does not want others to be hungry can have these profound and clear words. His "Reverence" and "Room Words" chapters bitterly denounced the crimes of despotism. Only someone who deeply hated the tyrants and traitors of the time and thoroughly understood that all the so-called sage emperors and virtuous kings were nothing more than that can have these insightful and pertinent words. In his "Room Words" chapter, he roughly stated that all emperors since the Qin Dynasty were thieves. Suppose someone is carrying several bolts of cloth and several bushels of grain on the road, and someone kills him and takes his cloth and grain. Is that a thief or not? Killing one person to take his cloth and grain is still called a thief, but killing all the people in the world and taking all their wealth is not called a thief? After the Three Dynasties, no one who gained the world was as good as the Han Dynasty, but Emperor Gao of Han massacred the cities of Chengyang and Yingyang, and Emperor Guangwu massacred three hundred cities. Encountering a village, he would destroy it; passing a market, he would disperse it; entering a city, he would massacre it. What was this for? The people died in war, and those who died because of war were fifteen or sixteen out of ten. The exposed bones were not yet collected, and the cries were not yet silenced. Then he would put on his robes, ride in his legal chariot, sit in the front hall, receive congratulations, build high palaces, and expand gardens to enrich his wives and concubines and fatten his sons and grandsons. What was his heart that he could bear to enjoy this? If God were to put me in charge of cases involving murder, I would know how to deal with them. Tang Zhen lived during the late Ming Dynasty, witnessing the changes in political power and the horrors of massacres and chaos. He once served in a new dynasty of a different ethnicity, but was submerged in a low position and could not reach the top, unable to express his talents and ambitions. Therefore, his resentful feelings were expressed in his writings, and he was unaware of the turmoil in his heart and the disturbance of his pen. Moreover, at that time, the Aisin Gioro clan had just entered China and was trying to impose a cruel government to intimidate the Chinese people. The Chinese people, under the oppression of violence, could do nothing for a while. Therefore, clever scholars sang praises of the status quo every day to please the barbarian dynasty and sought wealth and comfort for themselves. Those who were upright and self-respecting could only escape the status quo and retreat to the mountains and waters. Tang Zhen lived at that time. He could neither sing praises of the status quo and associate himself with the shameless, nor could he escape the status quo and call himself a remnant of the previous dynasty. In the beginning, he aspired to transform the status quo and intended to write "Heng Shu". In the end, he attacked the status quo with anger, and "Hidden Books" was completed. That is why I say his writing was born from resentment.

What does "learning from pleasure" mean? Tang's ability to endure hunger and cold and happily engage in writing has already been mentioned. In the "Pleasure Entry" chapter of "Hidden Books," he says, "The ancients had many methods of teaching, but I have not heard of teaching people with pleasure. Why do I enter through this? I am from Sichuan, and my nature is like its mountains and rivers, unable to tolerate turbulence, and I am always full of worries and anger. After careful observation, the root of my illness is that I am harmed by displeasure. Pleasure is my door, not the door of the masses." He also believed that without pleasure, one would often harbor annoyance, see much injustice, and see much irrationality. Therefore, all blaming of Heaven and others, and all lack of love, arise from this. Pleasure is the opposite. From this point of view, Tang's so-called pleasure is actually the pleasure of displeasure, born out of the helplessness of his situation and the words of a heartbroken man. Could he really be happy and pleased with himself? If he was happy and pleased with himself, and had no resentment, then there would be no need for him to write with anger, and "Hidden Books" would not have been written.

On the Bridge

— He Jiahua

On the bridge in Tanziwan, under the stone railing on the left, there is a peanut seller.

Except for rainy or snowy days when he has to carry an umbrella and a bamboo basket, running around selling, he sits under that stone railing, spreading old newspapers on the ground, dividing the peanuts into piles, waiting for customers.

He is very dull and doesn't know how to drum up business. He sits there all day, not even shouting a few times, just like a bodhisattva who doesn't speak.

"Three coppers for ten!"

When he sees someone walking by, he moves his lips, but only he can hear what he says. The peanuts he sells are not expensive, but here, even eating peanuts seems to be an extravagant pastime. Because business is slow, he is bored sitting there, constantly dividing the peanuts into piles, some with six, some with eight, and some with ten. Some days, he brings hundreds of peanuts from home, dividing them all day until evening, and not a single peanut is missing.

The area nearby is full of Jiangbei people who are so poor that they can't even afford to eat. They swarm like ants in small boats on the river, transporting manure, coal, and straw paper all day long, without a moment to spare. Their pigeon coops are next to the railway, with thatched roofs and yellow mud walls, swaying in the wind. When the train flies by, those thatched roofs are covered in a layer of dust. The housewives of these thatched huts have all left their families to work in factories. Their children are digging for coal cinders, carrying waste paper, and collecting broken bottles and pipes on the streets. Even though they are still little boys wearing red pants, they cannot be underestimated in their work.

The squalor here is especially shocking. The bridges, streets, and highways are filled with buses and jitneys speeding by all day long, raising clouds of dust. The small river evaporates a foul odor. In the distance, on that desolate wasteland, you can see countless open-air toilets, countless bottoms facing the sky, defecating there.

"Three coppers for ten!"

Sometimes he shouts louder, hoping to earn a few more coppers, but it is in vain. He has also reduced the price, selling twelve peanuts for three coppers, or twenty-four for just five coppers, but the people who pass by just look, shake their heads, as if these few coins are also a burden they cannot bear. They are all poor, miserable, and busy. It is rare for anyone to have a few coppers in their pockets and have the leisure to stand on the bridge, peel peanut shells, remove peanut skins, and eat peanut kernels.

The peanut seller sits there all day, like a bodhisattva who doesn't speak. Sometimes he gets bored and tries to chat with people:

"I really don't understand why there's no business!"

"It's natural not to eat peanuts, but it would be strange if no one got a haircut!"

The one who answers is a boy with a small braid on his head and a steel earring on his ear. Early in the morning, he brought a wooden frame with a washbasin, along with the razor and ear brush he inherited from his father, to the bridge to give haircuts. Business is also very slow.

"Haircuts? Humph, these Jiangbei people don't necessarily need that! They only get haircuts a few times a year. They won't think about getting a 'taro' (haircut) until their hair is like a 'long hair', and there are too many people doing your kind of business!"

Indeed, there are many barbers. Everywhere you can see wooden frames with washbasins, next to which sit people just as greasy, ragged, and covered in hair as this boy.

"Then even fewer people will eat your peanuts."

"It seems…but not necessarily…"

Hearing their conversation, a shoemaker who always has his sleeves rolled up, sitting on a leather stool not far away, suddenly interjects:

"Few people come to me to mend shoes either."

"That's for sure—" the peanut seller says sadly, "There aren't many Jiangbei people here who can afford shoes at all!"

"But…"

The shoemaker wants to say more, but seeing their sad faces, he has to remain silent. In fact, he also understands that in this slum, not only eating peanuts, but also getting haircuts and mending shoes seem unnecessary, wasteful expenses that can be cut.

Book Talk (Continued)

— Junzhi

(3)

In the "Tender Nest Notes," there is another story about a book lover, which reads: "Shaoshi Mountain Room Brush Collection: Zhang Wenqian's Keshan Collection of one hundred volumes. I saw a manuscript in a remote alley in Lin'an, with strange and ancient seals and elegant decorations. I was pleasantly surprised, but I had no money with me at the time. I looked around and found two pieces of green silk in my bag, and also took off the black silk straight gown and the half-sleeved blue Shu brocade I was wearing, and gave them all in exchange. The seller was distressed that he could not sell the book, and was overjoyed to get the price, so we agreed to meet the next morning. I returned to my residence and could not sleep all night. I visited him before washing my face and combing my hair in the morning, but found that a fire had spread from a neighbor's house the night before. This book was suddenly burned to ashes. To see a rare book and be willing to take off one's clothes, and even to be unable to sleep all night, can be said to be the ultimate in devotion to books. Unexpectedly, the god of fire played a prank and took it away in the night. How巧巧巧巧巧巧巧巧妙!?"

(4)

Huang Shangu said, "If a scholar does not read for three days, he feels that his language is tasteless, and even his face is hateful when he looks in the mirror." Mi Yuanzhang said, "If I don't read for a day, I feel my thoughts are dull." The book lover and the book are truly like air, which cannot be separated for a moment. And reading, in the eyes of the book lover, is not only to increase literary cultivation, but also an extremely beautiful painting, an extremely elegant life, as Chen Meigong's "Increased and Decreased Washing Brook Sand" says:

The fragrance of catalpa blossoms in the half-moonlight, the sound of oars returns as cicadas chirp, the curved willow bay with low thatched huts, hanging fish. Pointing and laughing at where my house is? A pond of lotus leaves, a small bridge across. Lamp light through paper windows in bamboo thickets, the sound of reading (see "Dwelling in Seclusion").

Two Brothers (72)

— Guy de Maupassant, translated by Li Wei Ke

She hugged him tightly and cried again: then she pressed her face against Zhan's face and continued, "Okay, but Bi'er? How can we be together with him!"

Zhan said in a low voice:

"We can always think of a way. You can no longer live with him."

The thought of her older son made her convulse in pain.

"No, I can't live with him anymore, can't! can not!"

She then threw herself into Zhan's arms, crying out as if she were facing a crisis in her mental state:

"Save me from him, my little baby, save me, think of a way, I don't know... look for it! Save me!"

"Okay, Mom, I'll think of a way."

"Immediately...it has to be immediately...you don't leave me! I'm afraid of him...I'm so scared!"

"Okay, I'll find a way. I promise you."

"Ah! But hurry, hurry! You really don't know what kind of feeling I have when I see him."

Then she whispered in his ear:

"Leave me here, leave me in your house."

He hesitated, thought about it with his precise mind, and he understood the danger of leaving his mother there.

But he had to explain to her for a long, long time, arguing with her, using clear arguments to overcome her frenzy and terror.

"Just leave me for one night," she said, "just leave me for tonight. Tomorrow you can tell Roland that I didn't come back because I was sick."

"That's impossible, because Bi'er has already returned. Come on, be brave. I will sort everything out tomorrow. I will be back home at nine o'clock tomorrow morning. Come on, put on your hat, I'll take you back."

"I'll do whatever you want," she said with a childlike attitude of fear and grateful faith.

She tried to get up; but the blow she had suffered was too great; she could not stand on her own legs.

So he gave her some sugar water, called for some alkali, and wiped her temples with vinegar. Like a woman after childbirth, she let him serve her.

He was finally able to walk, and she took his arm. As they passed the city hall, the clock was striking three.

In front of their residence, he embraced and kissed her, and said to her: "Goodbye, Mom, please be brave."

She secretly climbed the silent stairs, entered her bedroom, quickly took off her clothes, and, with the kind of feeling of excitement after a secret rendezvous, slipped in beside Roland, who was snoring.

In that house, only Bi'er was not asleep; he heard her return.

Special Publication for the Opening of the Entire Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway (Inscription by Lu Diping)

Inaugural Address

— Zeng Yangfu

Premier Sun Yat-sen's "The International Development of China" places railway construction as the backbone of all development plans. For the past five or six years, national affairs have been turbulent. The government and the public have been unable to actively construct this main railway line. All patriots in the country consider this a matter of regret. Now, the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway, only 350 kilometers long, has been completed, attracting the attention of Chinese and foreigners. The press has specially published a special issue to promote it during the opening ceremony. This is certainly not to show special favor to this short, light-rail railway. Rather, it is because a journey of a thousand miles begins with a single step, and a massive vehicle starts from a simple wheel. The high expectations reflect the heavy responsibilities placed upon it! We, the undersigned, are aware that today's achievements fall short of the expectations of our predecessors and society. However, we dare not conceal the three or four years of dedication and loyalty, nor the significant meaning of the construction of this railway. We must share it with the public.

Railways are the lifeline of transportation. They play a crucial role in promoting politics, regulating the economy, developing resources, and improving culture. When constructing railways today, it is necessary to consider the local financial strength, align with economic principles, meet social needs, and strive to embody the spirit of "doing everything possible quickly" advocated by contemporary talents. The construction funds for the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway did not come from loans alone; they were also raised from the people. If from loans, the cost must be minimized; if from the people, the burden must be as light as possible. Therefore, lightweight materials were used as much as possible, and all equipment was kept simple. This is not deliberately falling behind; it is because construction in China cannot proceed without doing everything possible. The purpose of building the railway is to facilitate transportation, and the railway itself must also be profitable. The section of the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway from the riverside to Lanxi took only two years to complete despite the many difficulties at the beginning. The section from Jinhua to Yushan was completed in just one year of rush construction. This is not a hasty approach. Rather, it is because railway construction must be done quickly to meet the needs of society. However, the significance of the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway goes beyond this.

China's construction today involves numerous aspects, far beyond the government's financial capacity. Moreover, society, accustomed to past failures of state-owned enterprises, lacks firm faith. Despite feeling an overabundance of capital, they dare not boldly invest in construction projects. This is a major reason why China's construction cannot proceed actively. However, the construction funds for the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway came from social investment, nearly half of the total. This created a new impression. In the future, the people can promote this spirit of mutual assistance and cooperation, enabling all construction projects to proceed simultaneously, leading China to prosperity and strength. The significance is immense!

Furthermore, due to China's vast territory and the obstruction of high mountains and great rivers, different regions have developed their own customs, making it difficult to achieve complete unity and solidarity. The completion of the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway is sufficient to solve the transportation difficulties of Zhejiang Province, but its northern end only starts at the opposite bank of Hangzhou, and its southern end only reaches Yushan in Jiangxi, neither of which connects with other railways. Its impact is limited to a small area. Looking at the national railway construction situation, the Yuehan (Guangzhou-Hankou) line is under active construction, but the completion of the Wuhan Railway Bridge is still uncertain. The natural barrier of the Yangtze River inspires only sighs of despair. The Beijing-Shanghai-Hangzhou line directly connects the north via the capital's ferry service, but the Qiantang River's tides pose a natural barrier to the east and west. To see a thousand miles, one must climb to a higher level! Fortunately, the construction of the Yushan-Pingxiang Railway is scheduled to begin, and the construction of the Qiantang River Bridge has also been planned. If we can promote the spirit of supporting the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway, with cooperation from the government and the public, then in the future, the Yushan-Pingxiang line can connect with the Yuehan Railway, and the Qiantang River Bridge can connect Beijing-Shanghai-Hangzhou and reach Tianjin-Pukou and Longhai. The northernmost border regions and the southernmost Guangdong will be connected, with goods transported in all directions. People from all regions will become one family. Once barriers are removed and autonomy is achieved, this small, 350-kilometer light-rail railway will have a great impact on the Republic of China and will contribute to the completion of the Premier's entire industrial plan. The excessive praise from society today has significant meaning! I, Yangfu, am unworthy, but I have followed in the footsteps of wise men, participated in this project, and strived to complete it, bringing me great honor. Therefore, I express my feelings at this moment.

Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway Jinhua River Bridge

Railway History

— Du Zhenyuan

Development

Inception: Zhejiang Province's deliberations on railway management began in 1927. At that time, the National Revolutionary Army's Northern Expedition pacified Jiangsu and Zhejiang. Mr. Zhang Jingjiang was ordered to preside over Zhejiang's administration. Recognizing that transportation in Zhejiang was limited by the mountainous terrain, he instructed the Construction Department to send personnel to survey the Zhejiang-Jiangxi and Zhejiang-Anhui routes, planning to build narrow-gauge light railways. With Mr. Zhang's departure from Zhejiang, the plan was shelved. In the winter of 1928, Mr. Zhang became the Director of the Construction Committee and concurrently administered Zhejiang. In December of that year, he telegraphed Zhenyuan to come to Zhejiang to re-survey the previously measured Zhejiang-Jiangxi route, starting from Zhakou in Hangzhou, following the left bank of the Qiantang River, passing through Fuyang, Tonglu, Jiande, Lanxi, Longyou, Qu County, and ending at Jiangshan, extending to Yushan in Jiangxi for communication. The entire route was approximately 317 kilometers long, surveyed over two months. On February 8, 1929, it was submitted to the 203rd meeting of the Zhejiang Provincial Government Committee, which resolved to build a railway according to the standard gauge, named the Zhejiang Provincial Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway.

Preparation: On February 25, 1929, the 207th meeting of the Zhejiang Provincial Government Committee approved the organizational outline of the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway Preparatory Office, appointing Zhenyuan as the director of the Preparatory Office. On March 1, the Preparatory Office was established, forming four survey teams to conduct preliminary and detailed surveys of the entire line in sections. On May 29, the Provincial Government Committee resolved that the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway Preparatory Office should be dissolved by the end of May, and an Engineering Bureau should be reorganized to commence planning for the railway project, appointing Zhenyuan as the Director and Chief Engineer of the Zhejiang Provincial Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway Engineering Bureau. On June 1, the Engineering Bureau was established. By July 29, the preliminary and detailed surveys by the survey teams were completed and submitted to the Bureau for mapmaking. At this time, the Provincial Government, due to the lack of construction funds for the railway, planned to issue 10 million yuan of Zhejiang Provincial Construction Bonds, designating 6.26 million yuan of these bonds for the construction of the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway. Although this was approved by the Central Government, the large amount of debt made it difficult to raise funds quickly, leading to a standstill.

Route Change: On December 10 of the same year, an order was received from the Provincial Government, instructing the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway Engineering Bureau to avoid the original personnel and manage the Xiaoshan-Changshan Highway project, transferring part of the funds allocated for railway construction to the construction of the Xiaoshan-Changshan Highway. In this situation, although the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway Engineering Bureau was not abolished in name, the actual construction was hopeless. Unexpectedly, the Construction Department proposed that the Xiaoshan-Changshan Highway was longer, passed through densely populated areas, and had abundant products. In addition to passenger transport, freight transport should also be considered. The highway could not bear the heavy burden of long-distance transport. It was proposed to follow this line, still using standard gauge, and replace it with a light railway to build the Xiaoshan-Changshan Light Railway, saving financial resources while gradually developing. This was approved by the Provincial Government Committee, and construction was to begin immediately. However, this line and the previously determined Hangzhou-Jiangshan route followed the same direction, with the only difference being that the Hangzhou-Jiangshan line followed the left bank of the river, while the Xiaoshan-Changshan line followed the right bank. Now that the right bank was being followed, the terrain between Xiaoshan, Zhuji, Yiwu, and Jinhua was flat and easy to construct. To implement the initial plan to connect to eastern Jiangxi, the northern section of the original Xiaoshan-Changshan route was extended to the opposite bank of Hangzhou, starting from the riverside, passing through Xiaoshan, Zhuji, Yiwu, Jinhua, crossing the Jinhua River, and changing to pass through Tangxi to reach Longyou. From Longyou westward, because there were commercially operated automobile roads between Quzhou, Changshan, and Yushan, and the mountains between Changshan and Yushan were steep, making construction difficult, it was decided to continue using the previously surveyed left bank section from Qu County to Jiangshan and extending to Yushan to save costs and avoid competition. This became the main line of the railway, with a length of 336 kilometers. A branch line was built from Jinhua to Lanxi, the Jinhua-Lanxi branch line, with a length of approximately 24 kilometers. After this change, the railway was no longer limited to Xiaoshan and Changshan as the beginning and end points. It was still called the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway to match the name with reality.

Engineering

Jiangshan-Lanxi Section: With the route of the railway determined, a groundbreaking ceremony was held on March 9, 1930. The section of the route between Xixing Riverside and Lanxi was divided into two main sections and six sub-sections, and construction began simultaneously. On December 14, track laying began between the riverside and Zhuji. Due to financial difficulties in the province, construction funds were insufficient, and orders were given to gradually advance each project in stages, slowing down the progress. After May 1931, the Provincial Government signed a loan contract with four Hangzhou banks, allowing work to be done more freely. On June 1, the railway was opened to traffic to Jianshan, and on July 1 to Zhuji. On October 1, track laying began again beyond Zhuji, and the railway was opened to traffic station by station as construction progressed. On March 6, 1932, the railway was opened to traffic to Lanxi. The Jiangshan-Lanxi section of the project was completed, entering the operational period. This section of the route was 195 kilometers long, and from the start of construction to completion, it took two years.

Jinhua-Yushan Section: The Jinhua-Yushan section of the project was supported by the Ministry and Council, which allocated Sino-British Boxer Indemnity funds for the purchase of foreign materials. The Provincial Government also obtained the consent of four Hangzhou banks to continue borrowing funds to complete the construction of the entire railway. On September 1, 1932, the detailed survey of the Jinhua-Yushan section of the route began, and a bridge survey team was formed to investigate the bridge sites along the line. After about two months, the survey was completed. To facilitate supervision, the section was divided into two main sections and eight sub-sections, and tenders were invited to construct the roadbed earthwork and stonework simultaneously. Construction began in November and December. Because the section had more large bridges than the Jiangshan-Lanxi section, the Jinhua River Bridge, in a crucial location, was the largest project and was tendered early, starting construction on December 20. Other bridges, large and small, were designed separately, and contractors were invited to build them, or the Bureau hired workers to do the work themselves. This section of the route was 165 kilometers long. Because the construction funds were raised at once, the project progressed rapidly. Track laying began in sections in July and August, reaching Jiangshan in early October. The railway was opened to traffic to Longyou on October 10, Qu County on November 1, and track laying reached Yushan on November 28, completing the entire line. From the start of construction to the present, it took only one year.

Capital

The entire railway was built in two phases. The funds raised by the Provincial Department and allocated as construction capital for the railway also came from different sources. The capital expenditures for the Jiangshan-Lanxi section and the Jinhua-Yushan section are described below:

(1) Jiangshan-Lanxi Section: This section of the route is 195 kilometers long. The actual construction cost was 6,939,516.43 yuan. The principal and interest repaid to the banking group was 316,244.25 yuan. The total capital income for both items was 7,255,760.68 yuan. Of this, 3.6 million yuan was allocated from loans twice from four Hangzhou banks (China, Zhejiang Xingye, Zhejiang Local, and China Agricultural and Industrial) with construction bond certificates and other items as collateral. 3,534,983.87 yuan was raised by the Provincial Department itself. The remaining 120,000 yuan was deducted by the banking group from the renewed loan for the construction of the Jinhua-Yushan section.

(2) Jinhua-Yushan Section: This section of the route is 165 kilometers long. The estimated construction cost was approximately 6.11 million yuan, divided into two categories: foreign material costs and domestic construction costs. The former was allocated 200,000 pounds from the Sino-British Boxer Indemnity. As of the end of June 1933, only 140,000 pounds of foreign materials had been purchased, equivalent to approximately 2.2 million yuan. The remaining 60,000 pounds was planned to be reserved for future expansion of equipment and was not included in the construction cost for this period. Due to limited funding sources, the latter was also estimated in two parts. Essential projects and equipment to maintain traffic were classified as the first part, requiring more than 3.56 million yuan. Projects related to increasing traffic safety and maintaining initial operations, which needed to be built as soon as traffic opened, were classified as the second part, requiring more than 340,000 yuan. The expenditure of this 340,000+ yuan was planned to be allocated monthly from operating income after traffic opened, saving initial construction capital. The domestic construction cost of the first part, 3.56 million+ yuan, was supplemented by the Provincial Government renewing the loan of 2.5 million yuan from the four Hangzhou banks that had previously provided the loan. The remaining amount was allocated from the surplus obtained from the sale of the Hangzhou Power Plant. The exact amount of capital expenditure for this section will be determined after the construction accounts are closed.

Operation

The Jiangshan-Lanxi section of the railway began handling passenger and freight transport on June 1, 1931, when traffic opened from Xixing Riverside to Jianshan. However, the operating equipment was not yet perfect, and

Page 18

Engineering Report

— Du Zhenyuan

Plan

The purpose of the railway's operation is to connect Zhejiang Province, and the route it passes through is originally a section of the southeastern railway system trunk line set by Dr. Sun Yat-sen: in the future, it can be extended to the major eastern ports and to Nanchang to connect the South China Sea Railway, and then to Pingxiang to connect with the Yuehan Railway. It can then directly reach Guangzhou. The importance of its location is evident. All engineering plans should be handled in accordance with the standards set by the Ministry to ensure long-term development. However, the railway's construction capital is raised by the Zhejiang Provincial Government itself. Due to financial constraints, it is impossible to carry out large-scale projects immediately. After careful consideration of the economic situation in eastern Zhejiang, there is currently no large amount of transport. Therefore, after studying the situation and adapting to local conditions, it was decided to adopt a light-rail wide-gauge plan to save initial construction capital. However, all designs have reserved space for improvement so that heavier rails can be replaced and heavier locomotives can be used in the future as the business develops. The railway's engineering plan is described in detail below:

(1) Steel Rails and Fittings: The railway's steel rails are 33 pounds per yard in weight. The length and cross-section, as well as the size of the rail spikes, are all handled in accordance with the regulations of the American Society of Civil Engineers. However, the fishplates and bolt holes are designed separately to increase the load on the track. The fishplates are 508 mm (20 inches) long, which is 98 mm (3 7/8 inches) longer than the original regulations. The center distance of the bolt holes is increased by 25 mm (1 inch) each, totaling 127 mm (5 inches), to be suitable for the suspended joint method.

(2) Track Gauge: The standard track gauge is still used to facilitate the replacement of heavier rails. The current cost is limited, and the benefits of future improvements are great.

(3) Rail Joint Method: The railway's roadbed is newly built, and the ballast is not fully laid. Rail joints are inevitably subject to subsidence from time to time. Therefore, the square joint method is used to prevent the track from becoming an extremely complex wave shape and to ensure smooth driving.

(4) Rail Sleepers: The sleepers are made of 150 mm (6 inches) square and 2440 mm (8 feet) long pine wood, with 14 sleepers laid per whole rail (9144 mm, i.e., 30 feet long). Initially, it was planned to use domestic products as much as possible. However, the local production was not abundant and could not be fully supplied. It was necessary to use foreign pine to meet urgent needs.

(5) Ballast: The railway's ballast requires 825 cubic meters per kilometer. The thickness below the sleepers is 200 mm (8 inches). The method of collecting materials is to open mountains along the line to collect ballast, or to pick up sand and ballast in nearby streams and rivers, or to select streams and rivers along the line with a large amount of pebbles, lay temporary branch lines to the shore to transport them, all using local materials to save costs and avoid waste.

(6) Roadbed: The soil quality along the railway is good. Therefore, the side slope of the embankment is generally set at a slope of 2:3. The width of the embankment varies depending on the height of the embankment. The width of the cut is uniformly 4 meters, with drainage ditches left on both sides for drainage. If the height of the cut and fill soil is more than 6 meters, a 1-meter wide bridle path is added in the middle of the side slope to prevent the side slope from collapsing and to protect the safety of the railway. As for the slope of the cut, it is determined differently depending on the geology: 1:1 for soil, 2:1 for soft rock, and 4:1 for hard rock. In addition, 2 to 4 meters of bridle paths are left under the foot of the slopes on both sides of the roadbed so that when the roadbed is widened in the future, there will still be appropriate bridle paths without repeated filling and excavation.

(7) Bridges: The railway's bridges are divided into three types: permanent, semi-permanent, and temporary construction, adapting to local conditions and considering the urgency, for economic facilities. Permanent bridges are mostly steel beam bridges, followed by concrete arch bridges, all designed according to the standard load set by the Ministry. Occasionally, the upper steel beams are temporarily designed according to Cooper's E35 load. Cover plates can be added at any time to increase the load-bearing capacity in the future. Semi-permanent bridges use concrete construction for the bridge seats and piers, also designed according to the permanent load-bearing capacity, while the upper structure is designed according to Cooper's E20 load. Old steel beams from the Shanghai-Hangzhou-Ningbo Railway are used, or wooden pile piers are temporarily added between the pier seats, with wooden beams erected on them, to facilitate future reconstruction. Temporary buildings are all wooden bridges, with the bridge seats and piers designed according to Cooper's E35 load, and the upper structure designed according to Cooper's E20 load. Wooden beams can also be added at any time to increase the load-bearing capacity.

(8) Track: The maximum curvature radius of the track is generally set at 300 meters. However, in special circumstances, to avoid large-scale projects, a smaller radius can be used, but it must not be less than 250 meters. The maximum slope, including the slope reduction force on the curve, is set at 1%. The superelevation of other curves and the design of sturdy curves, as well as the shortest limit of straight lines, are all handled in accordance with the standards of state-owned railways set by the Ministry.

(9) Station Facilities: The facilities of each station are based on the principle of simplicity and applicability to reduce initial construction costs. When the railway first opened, sleepers were used to build houses, or private houses were rented, or the most economical temporary houses were built as station buildings, or temples were temporarily borrowed as temporary ticket sales locations. However, efforts were made to build proper water supply and locomotive and passenger car houses, as well as platforms and rain sheds for the convenience of passengers.

(10) Locomotives and Vehicles: The choice of locomotives is based on the maximum traction force suitable for the railway's track. Freight cars use 15-ton capacity cars, and passenger cars focus on the equipment of third-class cars to ensure comfort and perfection. It is also planned to add third-class sleeper cars after the entire railway is open to meet the needs of long-distance passengers.

(11) Signals: At the beginning of the railway's operation, there were not many trains. Due to construction costs, train operations are temporarily notified by telephone and telegraph, with simple flags used as signals for trains entering and leaving the station. Now that business is gradually developing, in 1933, distant and near semaphore signals and departure semaphore signals will be added to each station in the Jiangshan-Lanxi section to ensure safety.

Jinyun-Lishui Road Matoushan Quarrying Work

Facilities

With the plan determined and the project implemented, except for steel rails, sleepers, locomotives, and passenger and freight car chassis, which must be purchased from abroad, the railway directly orders from foreign countries to avoid contractors from profiting from it. The remaining most of the projects are contracted out to facilitate simplicity. And for handling and supervising

A Bird's-Eye View of the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway

For ease of guidance, the Jiangshan-Lanxi section was divided into two main sections during construction, each with three sub-sections. Each sub-section was responsible for about thirty kilometers of track. The heads of the main and sub-sections were stationed within their respective jurisdictions to facilitate close supervision. After the completion of this section, the two main sections were merged into the first main section, and the sub-section system was abolished, with four work zones established to specialize in track maintenance. During the construction of the Jinhua-Yushan section, it was divided into the second and third main sections, each with four sub-sections, each responsible for approximately twenty kilometers. The general situation of the railway's engineering facilities is described below by category:

(1) Roadbed Construction: The contracting of roadbed earthwork and stonework projects was classified according to the nature of the soil and rock and the elevation, with unit prices as the standard, and the price was given after calculating the volume upon completion. The first bidding for the Jiangshan-Lanxi section was for the Jiangbian-Jianshan and Jianshan-Zhuji sub-sections. The second bidding was for the Zhuji-Suixi, Suixi-Yiwu, Yiwu-Jinhua, and Jinhua-Lanxi sub-sections. Xinmin Company contracted for the Jiangbian-Jianshan section, totaling approximately 220,000 cubic meters of earthwork and stonework. Datong Company contracted for the Jianshan-Zhuji section, totaling approximately 510,000 cubic meters of earthwork and stonework. Above Zhuji, a negotiated price method was adopted, and more than ten companies, including Daxing, Jinji, Zhao Chunji, Xiehe, and Yunsheng, were commissioned to do the work. The Jinhua-Yushan section was 164 kilometers long, with approximately 3.2 million cubic meters of earthwork and stonework. It was divided into eight sub-sections: Jinhua-Qianli, Qianli-Huzhen, Huzhen-Longyou, Longyou-Dongji, Dongji-Erlishijie, Erlishijie-Jiangshan, Jiangshan-Xintangbian, and Xintangbian-Yushan. Bids were invited for construction simultaneously, and contracts were signed immediately. Construction began successively in the winter of the 21st year and was completed successively in the autumn of this year. The progress of the project was much smoother than that of the Jiangshan-Lanxi section.

(2) Bridges and Culverts: Bidding for the bridges in the Jiangshan-Lanxi section eventually resulted in negotiated prices, and the construction was contracted to Yuqingchang, Shengzhongnan, Dongxing, Gengwu, and other companies separately. Construction began in May of the 19th year and continued, with all large and small bridges completed successively in the winter of the 20th year. There were a total of 100 bridges, including six iron bridges. Except for the wooden pile piers under the Jianshan River Bridge, which were temporary, the rest were concrete bridge abutments, with a total length of 177 meters and a total price of over 90,000 yuan. There were also 18 large and small concrete arch bridges, with a total length of 118 meters and a total price of over 160,000 yuan. There were also 76 large and small wooden bridges, with a total length of 1,217 meters and a total price of over 510,000 yuan. For the Jinhua-Yushan section's bridges and culverts, bidding was easier. Except for the culverts, which were installed by internal labor, the rest were contracted by selecting bidders through open bidding. There were 12 large bridges with a total length of over 1,600 meters, and 41 small bridges with a total length of 378 meters, requiring approximately 1.3 million yuan.

(3) Tracks: Due to financial constraints, the track materials for the entire railway were purchased in three batches. The first batch of 80 kilometers was for laying the Jiangbian-Zhuji section. The second batch of 144 kilometers was for the Zhuji-Lanxi section. The third batch of 180 kilometers was for laying the Jinhua-Yushan section. Therefore, the track laying project was also carried out in three phases. Initially, in mid-December of the 19th year, track laying began from Xixing Jiangbian, done by internal labor, and it was not until June of the following year that the tracks reached Zhuji. In October of the same year, the second batch of materials arrived, and the roadbed and bridge work above Zhuji had also been largely completed. Therefore, track laying continued above Zhuji, and it reached Lanxi in March of the 21st year. For the third phase of track laying in the Jinhua-Yushan section, a contract system was adopted. It began in late May of this year, and by November 28, the tracks were laid to the Yushan terminus, completing the entire railway. The roadbed of this railway was newly built and not yet solid, so track laying and opening to traffic were carried out immediately, resulting in ballast sinking constantly and often being insufficient. Furthermore, there were fewer vehicles; in addition to passenger and freight transport, there were only two ballast trains. By the end of June of this year, the ballast laid on the Jiangshan-Lanxi section was less than half of the original plan. Later, with the start of track laying in the Jinhua-Yushan section, the ballast cars were converted to transport materials, and the ballast laying work was stopped. The ballast for the Jinhua-Yushan section is now being laid only in the soft areas of the roadbed, not yet covering the entire area. Fortunately, the soil quality of the roadbed is still good, and trains can run safely at reduced speeds. As for the cost of ballast laying, it is approximately 920 yuan per kilometer for the Jiangshan-Lanxi section, and it is still difficult to calculate for the Jinhua-Yushan section, which is still in progress.

(4) Station Facilities: There are a total of 34 stations on the entire railway, including main and branch lines. In addition, Jingjiang Station was set up six kilometers west of Jiangbian Station, and Zhakou Station was set up on the opposite bank of Jingjiang Station to facilitate freight transport. A business station was set up at Sanlangmiao on the opposite bank of Jiangbian to facilitate passengers. The longest distance between stations is 27.5 kilometers, and the shortest is 5 kilometers. The grade of each station is determined based on the local population, products, and traffic conditions. Jiangbian is the starting point of the railway, with more frequent business. Jinhua is located in the middle of the entire line, at the beginning of the Jin-Lan branch line, and has the Jin-Wu-Yong-Jin highway for connection. Yushan is the terminus of the railway and a gathering area for the transfer of products from Zhejiang and Jiangxi. Therefore, their stations are all ranked first class. Linpu, Zhuji, Lanxi, Longyou, Quzhou, Jiangshan, and Hecun are either county towns, important towns, or places with abundant products, so they are all classified as second-class stations. The rest are classified as third or fourth-class stations, depending on local conditions. All construction at each station is simplified as much as possible due to financial constraints, focusing on practicality and avoiding extravagance, using local materials for design, or even renting civilian houses and temples as offices, as long as they are suitable for use, without hindering traffic safety or passenger convenience.

(5) Telegraph and Telephone: This project plans to use three pairs of No. 12 hard steel telephone wires and one iron telegraph wire. Due to the expense, it was necessary to outsource some work. Therefore, it was not possible to install everything at once. The Hangzhou Line Bureau to Jiangbian was initially engaged to supplement materials and requested the provincial telephone bureau to hang two pairs of No. 12 steel wires. Construction began on March 1 of the 19th year and was completed at the end of April. Due to the water line, it passed through Guanjia Weir across the river to Zhangjiacun, all using the telephone bureau's poles and submarine cables, totaling 37 kilometers. Zhangjiacun to Jiangbian was self-built and erected, totaling 6 kilometers. In total, this section is 43 kilometers long. Later, due to the long-term moisture exposure of the water line, the sound transmission was poor, which was inconvenient for both the telephone bureau and the railway. Therefore, they jointly purchased a ten-core submarine cable, each using half, totaling 700 meters, and replaced the equipment in the spring of the 22nd year. However, the telegraph line in this section is still lacking. The Jiangbian-Lanxi telephone line was self-installed by the railway in June of the 19th year with two pairs of No. 12 hard copper wires, reaching Lanxi in January of the 20th year. After the opening of traffic to Jianshan in June, due to the urgency of telecommunications, a telegraph line was added, completed in February of the 21st year. At that time, the opening of traffic to Lanxi was approaching, so a separate train service telephone line (No. 12 hard copper wire) was set up. Therefore, the Jiangshan-Lanxi section, including the incoming line, is 198 kilometers long. Exchange rooms were set up in Jiangbian, Zhuji, Yiwu, Jinhua, and other places, with a total of 65 telephones and 8 telegraphs. The total cost of labor and materials was 210,164 yuan (including the Hangzhou General Bureau to Jiangbian and the extension line, totaling 241 kilometers), with an average cost of 872 yuan per kilometer. Between Jinhua and Yushan, in order to ensure the smooth progress of the project, relying on telephone communication, poles were erected along the route in February of this year before the electrical materials were fully prepared, and one No. 8 galvanized iron wire was hung. It reached Huyan in late April. Due to insufficient materials, it was temporarily ended. The work lasted only 70 days, completing 156 kilometers of the main line and 8 kilometers of the incoming line, totaling 164 kilometers, with a total cost of 29,014 yuan. Later, the materials purchased with the Sino-British Boxer Indemnity arrived, and three pairs of telephone lines were continued to be hung in that section, which has recently been completed, and the previously hung iron wire was moved to be used as a telegraph line. The final settlement of all labor and material costs is currently being compiled.

(6) Locomotives and Rolling Stock: Domestically produced locomotives and rolling stock chassis are lacking and must be ordered from abroad. All projects such as riveting and car body assembly that can be done independently are designed and undertaken by the Xixing Machine Factory of the railway. However, the quantity is limited due to financial constraints, and only enough to meet current needs has been purchased. Currently in operation are nine locomotives, thirty passenger cars (with first, second, and third classes), and eighty freight cars (including covered wagons, open wagons, and flatcars). The speed of the locomotives is 50 kilometers per hour, with a maximum tractive force of 300 tons. Second and third-class passenger cars can accommodate 52 people, and freight cars have a load capacity of 15 tons each. The locomotives and rolling stock ordered with the Sino-British Boxer Indemnity have arrived with fifty freight car chassis, which will be successively made into covered wagons, open wagons, flatcars, etc., to join the transport. In addition, six locomotives and twenty-four passenger car underframes have not yet arrived. In total, there are fifteen locomotives, fifty-four passenger cars, and one hundred and thirty freight cars. If business develops in the future and these are insufficient, more will be added to meet the needs.

Summary

The scale of this railway project is vast, and its planning and facilities are not limited to the brief description above. Due to space limitations, only the most important aspects are summarized in this report. Detailed records have been compiled separately by the railway into a book called "Engineering Records," which will be distributed to guests from all walks of life at the opening ceremony in Jinhua today, and sent to important construction agencies and academic groups across the country for guidance and suggestions for improvement. Now, the construction cost of this railway will be briefly explained as a summary:

The total length of the entire railway is approximately 360 kilometers. The total construction cost is yet to be finalized for the Jinhua-Yushan section, as the project has just been completed, and a full settlement will take time. However, the final settlement for the Jiangshan-Lanxi section was over 7.2 million yuan, and the budget for the Jinhua-Yushan section was over 6 million yuan, totaling approximately 13 million yuan. The average cost per kilometer, including general affairs expenses, is approximately 36,000 yuan, which is less than half of that of other railways. Although light rails are temporarily used due to financial reasons, they are sufficient to meet current transportation needs and are suitable for the preliminary development needs of China's economy. Therefore, since the establishment of this railway, railways such as the Yu-Ping Railway in Jiangxi, the Long-Zhang Railway in Fujian, the Tong-Pu Railway in Shanxi, and the Cheng-Yu Railway in Sichuan have all planned to follow suit, in order to be light and easy to implement, so that society can understand that railway construction is not difficult, and the psychological fear of it in the past can be changed. This is what this railway is proud of.

Page 19

Business should be simplified. Therefore, with reference to the Ministry of Railways' General Rules for Passenger and Freight Transportation and considering the conditions of this railway, the Provisional Rules for Passenger and Freight Transportation of the Hangzhou-Jiangshan Railway in Zhejiang Province were drafted. Passenger transport initially only handled passenger and baggage transport, and freight transport was limited to cash payments. When the Jiangshan-Lanxi section was completed and opened to traffic in March of the 21st year, transportation of packages, livestock, vehicle bridges, currency, etc., was added from July 1st. From August 1st, handling of freight shipments with payment upon arrival began, and mail was carried from August 30th. On October 1st, passenger intermodal transport began with the Beijing-Shanghai and Shanghai-Hangzhou-Ningbo Railways. By April of the 22nd year, the Jiangshan-Lanxi section had been open to traffic for a full year. Business equipment such as station platforms and freight yards had been built in succession, and various businesses should be gradually increased. Therefore, it was approved that from July 1st, the Ministry's General Rules for Passenger Car Transportation of the Republic of China Railway, General Rules for Freight Car Transportation, General Rules for Responsible Freight Car Transportation, and Waybill Regulations would be fully applied. The previously established Provisional Rules for Passenger and Freight Transportation of this Railway were repealed at the same time. In light of the special circumstances of this railway, nine supplementary rules for passenger car transportation and eleven supplementary rules for freight car transportation were drafted and submitted for approval and promulgation. Also from July 1st, responsible freight intermodal transport began with the Beijing-Shanghai and Shanghai-Hangzhou-Ningbo Railways.

The operational revenue from the opening of traffic to Jianshan on June 1 of the 20th year to June 30 of the 22nd year, according to the fiscal year, listed as the 20th year (including revenue in June of the 20th year) and the 21st year, excluding miscellaneous revenue, is as follows:
